    [Original] Insufflation Unit Recall: Olympus Removes High Flow Insufflation Units
    The affected products and recommendations for what to do with the devices below have not changed. This recall involves removing certain devices from where they are used or sold. The FDA has identified this recall as the most serious type. This device may cause serious injury or death if you continue to use it. The FDA is aware that Olympus has issued a letter to affected customers recommending certain High Flow Insufflation Units be removed from where they are used or sold. Affected devices: Do not use affected devices. Identify and quarantine affected devices immediately. On January 16, 2026, Olympus sent all affected customers a letter recommending the following actions: Olympus stated that the software algorithm on High Flow Insufflation Unit, models UHI, UHI-2, and UHI-3, requires correction to address a potential issue which could lead to overpressure events. These products have been discontinued and have not been supported by repair for many years. Because no corrective solution is available for the affected models, Olympus has decided to remove these devices from the market. Over insufflation may lead to various patient harms during a procedure, including air embolism, arrythmias (bradycardia, asystole, or cardiac arrest), pneumothorax, kidney or urinary problems, hypoxia, subcutaneous emphysema, delay to treatment, more complex procedures, and potentially death. As of December 31, Olympus has reported two serious injuries, and no deaths associated with this issue. The UHI, UHI-2, and UHI-3 High Flow Insufflation Unit models are designed to insufflate the abdominal cavity and provide automatic suction and smoke evacuation to facilitate laparoscopic observation and treatment. Customers in the U.S. with adverse reactions, quality problems, or questions about this recall should contact Olympus at 1- 800-848-9024 and select option 1. Health care professionals and consumers may report adverse reactions or quality problems they experienced using these devices to MedWatch: The FDA Safety Information and Adverse Event Reporting Program.

    [Original] Fortress sells FDA voucher for $205M after Zycubo approval last month
    Feb 23, 2026 10:15am priority review voucher FDA Fortress Biotech Rare Pediatric Disease Priority Review Voucher --> Only a month after Jazz Pharmaceuticals said it had signed a deal to sell an FDA priority review voucher (PRV) for $200 million, a new PRV transaction involving Fortress Biotech and an unnamed buyer shows that the trend of rising voucher prices is still going strong. Monday morning, Fortress said its subsidiary, Cyprium Therapeutics, has entered into an agreement to sell a recently received rare pediatric disease priority review voucher for $205 million. Cyprium got its hands on the PRV as part of the FDA's recent approval of injected copper replacement therapy Zycubo as the first treatment approved in the U.S. for the rare neurodegenerative disorder Menkes disease. While another company, Sentynl Therapeutics, is handling development and commercialization of Zycubo under a 2023 agreement, the deal called for Sentynl to transfer the PRV back to Fortress/Cyprium after the approval. Following the Jan. 12 approval of Zycubo, Cyprium wasted no time in striking a PRV sale. The $205 million transaction follows last month's disclosure from Jazz that it had agreed to sell its own PRV for $200 million. Before that, PRV transfers last year ranged between $150 million and $175 million. PRVs are awarded in conjunction with certain rare pediatric disease approvals and enable users to shorten FDA reviews from the agency's standard 10-month window to about six months. For a company with a promising launch waiting in the wings, a PRV could be worth hundreds of millions of dollars in order to help speed a highly anticipated drug to market. In the case of Cyprium and Zycubo, the company said it remains eligible for tiered royalties based on the drug's sales and up to $129 million in milestones. Further, the company stressed that it must pay 20% of the PRV sale proceeds to the Eunice Kennedy Shriver National Institute of Child Health and Human Development. The trend of increasing PRV sale values comes even as the FDA advances a new initiative called the commissioner's national priority voucher. Winners of these vouchersâ€”which are handed out at the agency's discretion based on certain national prioritiesâ€”are entitled to ultrafast FDA reviews spanning one to two months. The controversial program has already yielded one rejection, with Disc Medicine's bitopertin being spurned earlier this month.

    [Original] Small and mighty: single-domain antibodies pack a biological punch
    VHH antibodies, also known as singleâ€‘domain antibodies (sdAbs) or nanobodies, are exceptionally small, highly stable antibody fragments that are attracting increasing attention for their unique structural and functional advantages over conventional IgG antibodies. Their discovery began in 1989 with the identification of heavyâ€‘chainâ€‘only antibodies in the Camelidae family, which revealed the variable antigenâ€‘binding domain (VHH). The first FDAâ€‘approved VHH therapeutic, Caplacizumab (CabliviÂ®), received approval in 2019, marking a milestone that has since accelerated the development of additional programs. By the end of 2025, five VHH therapeutics have received global approval, and more than 49 distinct VHH sequences are advancing through clinical development. The global VHH antibody market is expanding rapidly, with compound annual growth rate (CAGR) estimates around 15%. This growth is driven by the technologyâ€™s promise in oncology, immunology, and diagnostic imaging, where its small size and stability can be leveraged for improved tissue penetration and barrier crossing, including the bloodâ€“brain barrier. VHHs are typically 12â€“15 kDa, roughly oneâ€‘tenth the size of conventional IgGs, allowing them to access cryptic epitopes that larger antibodies cannot reach. Structurally, VHHs consist of a single variable domain, which simplifies folding and confers exceptional thermal and chemical stability. This robustness enables use in challenging formulation environments, such as respiratory and digestive systems, and supports efficient manufacturability. The absence of a light chain grants VHHs remarkable engineering flexibility; they can be fused with effector domainsâ€”including enzymes, toxins, and Fc regionsâ€”to create highly functional, customizable molecules. Their modular nature also facilitates the creation of multispecific formats, making them attractive building blocks for complex therapeutics. Manufacturing VHHs can be performed in simpler expression systems such as Escherichia coli and yeast, reducing production timelines and overall process development costs compared with mammalianâ€‘cellâ€‘based production required for fullâ€‘length antibodies. However, the lack of wellâ€‘established chemistry, manufacturing, and controls (CMC) pathways for VHHs, unlike the decades of GMP practice and regulatory clarity for IgGs, can prolong development timelines. Challenges also arise in library generation and diversity, where sequence diversity loss, difficulty conducting functional assays, and extended timeframes can impede progress. Process development and scaling present additional hurdles. The small size of VHHs increases the risk of loss during filtration and ultrafiltration steps, and tailored analytical methods may be necessary to ensure product quality. Platform fit and expression system compatibility further complicate scaling efforts. Despite these challenges, VHH antibodies hold strong scientific momentum. As one industry voice notes, â€œVHH antibodies: theyâ€™re not just small; they are modular engineering platforms. The only limit is our imagination!â€ Commercial success will hinge on overcoming the distinct discovery, humanization, expression, and analytical characterization challenges that accompany this emerging technology.

    Internal investigators at Binance uncovered a series of potentially illegal transactions last year, revealing that Iranian actors had accessed more than 1,500 Binance accounts and that roughly $1.7 billion was transferred from two Binance accounts to Iranian entities linked to terrorist groups. One of the accounts involved was a Binance vendor. After the findings were reported to senior executives, Binance fired or suspended at least four employees involved in the investigation, citing â€œviolations of company protocolâ€ related to client data handling. The timing of these disciplinary actions, shortly after the Iranian transaction alerts, raised questions about whether they were connected to the investigatorsâ€™ findings. Binance, the worldâ€™s largest cryptoâ€‘trading venue, had previously pleaded guilty in 2023 to violating antiâ€‘moneyâ€‘laundering laws and U.S. sanctions, agreeing to pay a $4.3 billion penalty and to notify authorities of any illicit activity. The company had hired more than 60 compliance staff with lawâ€‘enforcement experience to address the problem. Despite these measures, internal warnings about Iranian transactions surfaced in the months before President Trump granted a pardon to Binance founder Changpeng Zhao, who had spent four months in federal prison in 2024 for his role in the firmâ€™s crimes. Zhao, who remains the companyâ€™s majority shareholder and is worth nearly $80 billion, was a guest at a Marâ€‘aâ€‘Lago conference this month, where he posted a photo and the caption â€œLearned a lot.â€ Binance investigators traced the $1.7 billion to a Hong Kongâ€‘based business called Blessed Trust, owned by companies incorporated in the British Virgin Islands and the Cayman Islands. Blessed Trust operated as a fiat partner to Binance, handling payments and payroll for Binance entities. Over the past two years, investigators found $1.2 billion in crypto flowed from Blessed Trustâ€™s Binance account to Iranianâ€‘linked entities, some tied to the U.S.â€‘designated terrorist group Islamic Revolutionary Guards Corps. Binanceâ€™s representative Rachel Conlan stated that the exchange removed the accounts linked to the $1.7 billion and notified authorities, asserting that no evidence of sanctions violations was found. Conlan also said that the investigators were not suspended or terminated for raising compliance concerns, but that â€œcertain individualsâ€ were disciplined for â€œunauthorized disclosure of confidential client information.â€ Other investigations revealed that a defunct Hong Kong business, Hexa Whale Trading Limited, had used Binance to send $490 million to crypto wallets tied to Iranian entities, allegedly financing terrorist organizations such as the Houthis. Binance notified the Justice Department in October and removed Hexa Whale from the exchange. The company also identified that a fleet of Russian cargo ships, believed to be evading sanctions, used Binance accounts to pay crew members, but Conlan said the activity did not constitute a sanctions violation. The internal disciplinary actions followed the departure of more than half a dozen compliance officials, including a sanctions manager and the head of enterprise compliance. Binanceâ€™s chief compliance officer, Noah Perlman, has also discussed leaving. The White House press secretary, Karoline Leavitt, described Zhaoâ€™s prosecution as part of a â€œwar on cryptocurrencyâ€ by the previous administration, claiming it damaged the U.S.â€™s reputation as a technology leader. Binanceâ€™s investigations continue, with other employees working on the cases despite the suspensions.

    Washington Park High School in Newark has launched a new elective on artificialâ€‘intelligence literacy, treating the technology like a vehicle that students must learn to drive responsibly. The fourâ€‘story charter school, part of Uncommon Schools and serving about 900 students, hired careerâ€‘exploration teacher Mike Taubman and U.S. history teacher Scott Kern to design the course. Both educators had already integrated AI tools into their regular classes and now aim to give seniors a â€œdriverâ€™sâ€‘licenseâ€ framework for navigating AI. The first session, held this month, began with a simple assignment: students compared moments when they passively scrolled through AIâ€‘driven socialâ€‘media feeds to times when they actively chose the videos or search results they wanted. â€œAre you steering the technology or is it steering you?â€ the slide on the whiteboard asked. 18â€‘yearâ€‘old Adrian Farrell said he had taken charge by asking a chatbot to check his math homework. Brianna Perez, also 18, described herself as in â€œpassenger modeâ€ when using Spotifyâ€™s AI DJ, which â€œplays your favorite music so you donâ€™t have to change it.â€ Taubman likened the class to preparing teenagers for a driverâ€™sâ€‘license exam. â€œWhere do you want to go, and can AI help you get there?â€ he asked. Students learn to analyze AI tools, develop personal guidelines, and design safety policies. Kern added, â€œAnytime we want kids interacting with each other or doing initial critical thinking, I would never want AI or any sort of technology of that ilk to interfere with that.â€ The curriculum covers practical usesâ€”prompting Googleâ€™s Gemini or Microsoftâ€™s Copilotâ€”and societal impacts, such as the spread of AIâ€‘generated nude images, known as deepfakes. It also examines the ethics of AIâ€‘generated film scenes, with students debating whether humans should still receive credit when AI creates visual content. Kern noted that the New York Times has sued OpenAI and Microsoft over copyright claims, and both companies deny wrongdoing. The course also includes handsâ€‘on projects. In a recent AP U.S. History class, Kern had students read 1919 Chicago Race Riot newspaper clippings, then use a custom chatbot to refine their arguments. A Cambridge University and Microsoft Research study found that students who took notes on text passages had better reading comprehension than those who relied on chatbots. The Brookings Institution last month concluded that the risks of AI in education currently outweigh its benefits.

    [Original] Pentagon Summons Anthropic Chief in Dispute Over A.I. Limits
    Pentagon officials have summoned Anthropic chief executive Dario Amodei to Washington for a meeting on Tuesday to discuss how the companyâ€™s artificialâ€‘intelligence technology is used on classified military systems. The Defense Department and Anthropic had agreed to a $200 million pilot contract last year, but a January 9 memo from Secretary of Defense Pete Hegseth calling on AI firms to remove restrictions on their models prompted the two sides to renegotiate the terms. The Pentagon has already signed an agreement with Elon Muskâ€™s xAI and is close to striking a deal with Google, which develops the Gemini model, according to people briefed on the discussions. Defense officials hope that those agreements will give them leverage to pressure Anthropic into allowing its model to be used more broadly. Google and xAI did not immediately respond to requests for comment. A Defense Department official declined to comment on any future announcements but confirmed that Hegseth would meet with Amodei at the Pentagon. Anthropic will be asked to agree to the same guardrails that the Department is negotiating with other AI companies. In those negotiations, the Pentagon has said the contracts must allow the department to use the models as it sees fit, provided the activities are lawful, while allowing the companies to build safety provisions into their modelsâ€”what the firms call â€œthe safety stack.â€ Anthropic was the first company authorized to work on the militaryâ€™s classified networks. The company said it was willing to loosen its restrictions but has demanded that guardrails be put in place to stop its AI from being used for mass surveillance of Americans or deployed in autonomous weapons that have no humans in the loop, people involved in the discussions said. Those close to Anthropic argue that the startup has taken more care than its rivals to keep its technologies out of the hands of Chinese companies. In November, the company said it had banned a Chineseâ€‘stateâ€‘sponsored group that was using its technologies in a hacking campaign targeting large tech firms, financial institutions, chemical manufacturers and government agencies. Earlier last year, OpenAI, another leading AI company whose model is used on unclassified military networks, said it had discovered and worked to stop two different Chinese campaigns to use AI for surveillance. (The New York Times has filed a lawsuit against OpenAI and Microsoft, its partner.) Ahead of the meeting with Hegseth on Monday, Anthropic published a blog post stating that three Chinese AI companies siphoned information from Anthropic to try to improve their own models. Pentagon officials have acknowledged that removing Anthropic from the classified system would cause shortâ€‘term disruptions. Experts say that military service members regularly use Anthropic along with technology from Palantir, a dataâ€‘analytics company, to analyze classified data, and that cutting the military off from Anthropicâ€™s AI chatbot, Claude, would be counterproductive. The xAI model is not considered as advanced or as reliable as Anthropicâ€™s, while Googleâ€™s Gemini is considered a rival to Anthropic and OpenAI. Google is eager to strike a deal; it has spent heavily on data centers to be used exclusively by the government, but that computing capacity has so far been underused. OpenAI is not close to a deal. People briefed on the negotiations say the company believes it needs to continue working on its safety technology before its model can be used on classified networks. The meeting between Hegseth and Amodei was reported earlier by Axios. Cade Metz contributed reporting from San Francisco. Julian E. Barnes covers U.S. intelligence agencies and international security matters for The Times. Sheera Frenkel is a reporter based in the San Francisco Bay Area, covering how technology affects everyday lives with a focus on social media companies.

The AI boom follows a familiar pattern of early optimism, speculative investment, and eventual skepticism. Vincent Mosco, a technology historian, wrote in *The Digital Sublime* that each generation renews the belief that the latest technology will fulfill radical promises.     [Original] Hong Kong stocks retreat on AI worries, China markets rally in Year of the Horse
    Tech heavyweights drag Hong Kong stocks lower as investors fret over AI disruption following Wall Streetâ€™s decline Hong Kong stocks fell on Tuesday, tracking Wall Streetâ€™s losses, as renewed concerns about the artificial intelligence race weighed on sentiment, while mainland markets rallied on the first trading day in the Year of the Horse.The Hang Seng Index dropped 0.6 per cent to 26,913.68 at the open, paring some of the 2.5 per cent gain recorded on Monday. The Hang Seng Tech Index fell 1 per cent. On the mainland, the CSI 300 Index rose 1.4 per cent while the Shanghai Composite Index jumped 1.2 per cent. Technology heavyweights led the major losers. Chipmaker Semiconductor Manufacturing International Corporation dropped 1.7 per cent to HK$69.90, and search-engine firm Baidu slid 1.6 per cent to HK$131.10. E-commerce major Alibaba Group Holding fell 1.5 per cent to HK$149.90, and short-video platform operator Kuaishou Technology slipped 1.5 per cent to HK$67.50. Limiting losses, insurance firm AIA rose 1.5 per cent to HK$86.95 and power-tool maker Techtronic Industries gained 1.5 per cent to HK$125.70. PetroChina added 1.5 per cent to HK$9.71 and gold miner Zijin Mining advanced 0.8 per cent to HK$45.26. Among other major Asia-Pacific markets, Japanâ€™s Nikkei 225 gained 0.9 per cent and South Koreaâ€™s Kospi climbed 1.1 per cent, while Australiaâ€™s S&P/ASX 200 slipped 0.2 per cent.

    [Original] Company director jailed over Â£7m airline parts fraud
    Jose Alejandro Zamora Yrala, 38, has been sentenced to four years and eight months in prison for fraud after a 2023 investigation by the Serious Fraud Office (SFO) uncovered that his aircraft parts company, AOG Technics, supplied an estimated 60,000 engine components to airlines and maintenance firms between 2019 and 2023 using forged documents. The parts, valued at nearly Â£7 million, were intended for CFM56 engines â€“ the worldâ€™s bestâ€‘selling aircraft engine used on Boeing 737s, Airbus A320s and other aircraft. Zamora Yrala operated AOG Technics from a home office in Surrey, selling parts directly to airlines, repairâ€‘maintenanceâ€‘overhaul (MRO) providers and other brokers. The fraud was exposed when an engineer at Portuguese carrier TAP questioned the origin of a part that could not be fitted; the manufacturer confirmed the documentation was fake. The discovery triggered a global grounding of aircraft to inspect engines and replace suspect parts, affecting carriers such as Ryanair, American Airlines, Ethiopian Airlines and TAP Air Portugal. The SFO estimates the total losses at Â£39.3 million. The court heard that the companyâ€™s Authorised Release Certificates (ARCs), which certify that new or refurbished parts are airworthy, were falsified. Some certificates were genuine but had details altered on a home computer by an accomplice who worked as a technician at an airline; others were forged with the help of a Spanish graphic designer. In addition, Zamora Yrala produced fake documents purporting to show where AOG Technics had sourced the parts, and invented fictitious employees. Emails and documents were signed by fabricated sales and quality managers, creating the illusion of a legitimate business. Only Zamora Yrala, his thenâ€‘wife, her brother and the familyâ€™s nanny appeared on the payroll. Judge Simon Picken, delivering the sentence, said Zamora Yralaâ€™s actions constituted a â€œmore or less complete underminingâ€ of the rules and regulations that ensure passenger flight safety. He added that the fraud â€œalmost impossible to identify where parts sold with forged certificates came from,â€ echoing SFO case controller Harriet Sassoonâ€™s earlier statement. Alongside the prison term, Zamora Yrala will be disqualified from acting as a company director for eight years and will face proceedsâ€‘ofâ€‘crime proceedings later this year. A related investigation by Portuguese authorities remains ongoing. The case underscores the critical importance of stringent oversight in the highly regulated aircraft parts supply chain.

    [Original] When is the Spring Statement and what might be in it?
    Chancellor Rachel Reeves will deliver the UK Spring Statement on 3 March, providing updated forecasts for growth, inflation, unemployment, government spending and tax income for the coming years. While not as highâ€‘profile as the Budget, the statement can shape future tax and spending decisions. The Office for Budget Responsibility (OBR) will publish its latest economic forecasts after Reevesâ€™ speech, offering an independent assessment of whether the government is likely to meet its fiscal rules. At the November Budget, the OBR projected that Reeves would meet the first fiscal rule with Â£21.7 bn in reserveâ€”often called â€œheadroom.â€ The OBRâ€™s forecasts are crucial because the government may cut spending or raise taxes if it risks breaching its rules. Historically, the OBR assessed rule compliance twice a year, in spring and autumn, but the government announced in November that this assessment would now occur only once a year at the autumn Budget. The 2026 Spring Statement will therefore be the sole annual review for the year. Reeves is not expected to announce major policy changes such as tax or spending adjustments; those are typically reserved for the autumn Budget. However, the statement may confirm or reverse earlier measures. Last yearâ€™s statement confirmed changes to benefits, some of which were later reversed. The opposition will likely respond after Reeves, with either Conservative leader Kemi Badenoch or shadow chancellor Mel Stride speaking. The OBRâ€™s March forecast will be published by the Treasury on the gov.uk platform following a security review after last yearâ€™s premature release of the OBRâ€™s analysis, which led to the resignation of OBR chairman Richard Hughes. The Treasury will release the forecast shortly after Reevesâ€™ speech, which is expected to begin shortly after midday. Labourâ€™s economic agenda, set in motion when the party took power in July 2024, prioritises growth. Yet many economists and politicians worry the UK economy is not expanding fast enough. GDP grew by 0.1 % in the last three months of 2025, slightly below expectations, and 1.3 % over the full year. Inflation, still above the Bank of Englandâ€™s 2 % target, peaked at 11.1 % in October 2022 but fell to 3 % in the year to Januaryâ€”the lowest rate since March 2025. This decline has led analysts to anticipate a Bank of England rate cut from 3.75 % at its next meeting. Unemployment rose slowly, reaching 5.2 % in the three months to December, the highest rate in nearly five years. Wage growth slowed, yet average pay still outpaced inflation; wages excluding bonuses grew at an annual rate of 4.2 % in the three months to December. In February, Reeves said 2026 would be the year the public begins to feel the positive impacts of Labourâ€™s changes. â€œIs there more to do? Absolutely. But weâ€™ve created the conditions for growth and I am confident this will be the year we will see the results of that,â€ she said. Business owners continue to voice concerns over rising tax burdens, particularly the employer National Insurance contribution hike that took effect last April, which has increased hiring costs.

    [Original] Unlicensed betting firms face sponsorship ban
    Image source, Getty ImagesImage caption, Bournemouth are one of several Premier League clubs sponsored by betting websites run by TGP Europe Gambling firms not licensed in the UK could be banned from sponsoring sports teams - including Premier League clubs - as part of a government crackdown. Ministers say they are worried about the risks stemming from the unlicensed market, such as companies not following laws and guidelines aimed at protecting customers. These include mandatory financial vulnerability checks and responsible advertising. The government also warns a lack of data protection measures can leave people open to fraud and identity theft and that unlicensed betting has been linked to organised crime. A consultation into the prosposals will be launched this spring. Last year, several clubs were warned by the Gambling Commission over their relationship with unlicensed TGP Europe. Premier League Bournemouth, Fulham, Newcastle and Wolves, as well as then-Championship Burnley, were all sponsored by betting websites run by the firm, which had surrendered its British licence after an investigation found it failed to "carry out sufficient checks on business partners" and breached "anti-money laundering rules". A letter sent to the clubs warned they "may be liable to prosecution... if they promote unlicensed gambling businesses that transact with consumers in Great Britain". Announcing the forthcoming consultation, Culture Secretary Lisa Nandy said people placing bets "deserve to know the sites they're using are properly regulated, with the right protections in place". She added: "It's not right that unlicensed gambling operators can sponsor some of our biggest football clubs, raising their profile and potentially drawing fans towards sites that don't meet our regulatory standards." In a post on social media site X, the Betting and Gaming Council backed the government's plan, writing: "It's not right that gambling companies without a UK licence can sponsor Premier League clubs. "If an operator wants the visibility and credibility of English football, they should meet the high regulatory standards set here in the UK." Premier League to limit gambling sponsors on shirts Published13 April 2023From beer to betting - how have football shirt sponsors changed? Published18 April 2025Four Premier League clubs warned over gambling sponsors Published16 May 2025Premier League clubs have previously collectively agreed to withdraw gambling sponsorship from the front of their matchday shirts by the end of this season. However, sleeve sponsorship can still carry gambling branding, including that of unlicensed firms, and the government warns that can give non-regulated companies "a major presence in the country's most-watched sports league". Ministers argue there is a "strong case for stopping unlicensed sponsorship altogether given the brand visibility could drive consumers towards unlicensed sites operating outside the Gambling Commission's regulatory protections". The commission estimates up to 1.4 million adults in the UK have a gambling problem, external. TGP Europe has been approached for comment. Get news, insight and fan views on your Premier League team

    [Original] Pentagon Summons Anthropic Chief in Dispute Over A.I. Limits
    Pentagon officials have called in Anthropicâ€™s chief executive, Dario Amodei, to Washington for a meeting with Defense Secretary Pete Hegseth on Tuesday to discuss how the companyâ€™s artificialâ€‘intelligence technology is used on classified military systems. The meeting follows a $200 million pilot contract that the Department and Anthropic signed last year, and a January 9 memo from Hegseth urging AI firms to lift restrictions on their models. The memo prompted the two sides to renegotiate the contract. The Department has already signed an agreement with Elon Muskâ€™s xAI and is close to striking a deal with Google, which develops the Gemini model, according to people briefed on the talks. Defense officials say the new agreements will give them leverage to pressure Anthropic into allowing its model to be used more broadly. Google and xAI have not yet responded to requests for comment. A Defense Department official, who declined to comment on future announcements, confirmed that Hegseth will meet with Amodei at the Pentagon. Anthropic will be asked to agree to the same guardrails that the Department is negotiating with other AI companies. In those negotiations, the Pentagon has said contracts must allow the department to use the models as it sees fit, provided the activities are lawful. The companies, however, are allowed to build safety provisions into their models, which they call a â€œsafety stack.â€ Anthropic was the first AI firm authorized to work on the militaryâ€™s classified networks. The company said it is willing to loosen its restrictions but has demanded that guardrails be put in place to prevent its AI from being used for mass surveillance of Americans or deployed in autonomous weapons without human oversight, according to people involved in the discussions. Anthropic has also argued that it has taken more care than its rivals to keep its technologies out of the hands of Chinese companies. In November, the startup said it had banned a Chineseâ€‘stateâ€‘sponsored group that was using its technologies in a hacking campaign targeting large tech firms, financial institutions, chemical manufacturers and government agencies. Earlier last year, OpenAI, whose model is used on unclassified military networks, said it had discovered and stopped two separate Chinese campaigns that sought to use AI for surveillance. The New York Times has filed a lawsuit against OpenAI and its partner Microsoft. Ahead of the meeting, Anthropic published a blog post claiming that three Chinese AI companies had siphoned information from its models to improve their own systems. Pentagon officials acknowledge that removing Anthropic from the classified system would cause shortâ€‘term disruptions. Experts note that military service members regularly use Anthropicâ€™s chatbot, Claude, alongside Palantirâ€™s dataâ€‘analytics tools to analyze classified data, and that cutting the military off from Claude would be counterproductive. The Department hopes that the pending deals will give it leverage. The xAI model is not considered as advanced or reliable as Anthropicâ€™s, while Googleâ€™s Gemini is seen as a rival to both Anthropic and OpenAI. Google is reportedly eager to strike a deal and has invested heavily in data centers that could be used exclusively by the government, though that capacity has so far been underused. OpenAI is not close to a deal, according to people briefed on the negotiations; the company believes it needs to continue working on its safety technology before its model can be used on classified networks. The meeting between Hegseth and Amodei was reported earlier by Axios. Cade Metz contributed reporting from San Francisco. Julian E. Barnes covers U.S. intelligence agencies and international security matters for The Times, and Sheera Frenkel reports on technologyâ€™s impact on everyday life, focusing on social media companies.

    [Original] After Saks Bankruptcy, Richard Baker Says He Saved Luxury Department Stores
    Richard Baker, the former chairman of Saks Global, set out to create a luxury retail empire by merging Saks Fifth Avenue, Neiman Marcus and Bergdorf Goodman.     [Original] More than 5,000 flights cancelled as US east coast digs out of record snow
    A powerful winter storm struck the U.S. east coast on Monday, delivering recordâ€‘breaking snowfall and crippling travel and power infrastructure across several states. The National Weather Service reported that parts of Rhode Island and Massachusetts received nearly 37 inches (94 cm) of snow, while New York Cityâ€™s Central Park logged more than 19 inches (48 cm). The stormâ€™s intensity left travel conditions in New York â€œnear impossible,â€ according to a state agency warning. More than 600,000 homes and businesses on the east coast suffered power outages, with New Jersey and Massachusetts reporting the worst disruptions. Winter storm warnings extended from North Carolina to northern Maine, and some alerts reached eastern Canada. The National Weather Service said the snow would continue through Monday, with some coastal areas expected to accumulate 1 ft to 2 ft (61 cm) by Tuesday morning. Rhode Island, the smallest state, endured the heaviest snowfall, setting a new state record. Providence received 36 inches of snow, surpassing the previous singleâ€‘storm record of 28.6 inches set in February 1978. â€œIt completely smashed it,â€ said Candice Hrencecin, a National Weather Service meteorologist in Boston, in an interview with the New York Times. â€œWe were just as shocked as everyone else.â€ In response, Rhode Island and neighboring Connecticut imposed bans on nonâ€‘essential travel. Later that day, Massachusetts Governor Maura Healey issued a travel ban for all nonâ€‘essential driving in southeastern Massachusetts and lowered the speed limit on the Pike to 40 mph (25 km/h). â€œWhiteout conditions are making travel extremely dangerous,â€ she posted online. â€œIf you get stuck, help will have a hard time reaching youâ€¦ I strongly urge everyone to stay off the roads no matter where you live.â€ Power outages in Massachusetts affected nearly 300,000 customers, including 85 % of those in Barnstable County, which covers all of Cape Cod. In New York City, a travel ban brought the cityâ€™s 8 million residents to a near standstill before it was lifted at noon local time. All roads, highways, and bridges were shuttered. Connecticut and New Jersey faced concerns over falling trees and branches that could create hazardous road conditions and further outages. In Providence, a city spokesman told The Providence Journal that more than 300 vehicles had been towed for blocking snowploughs. Tow operations halted by the afternoon, and drivers shifted to helping motorists whose cars had become stuck. Air travel was severely impacted. FlightAware recorded more than 5,706 flights canceled within, into, or out of the United States. At LaGuardia Airport, 98 % of flights were canceled; at JFK, 91 % were canceled. Bostonâ€™s Logan International Airport canceled 92 % of outgoing flights, Newark Liberty Airport in New Jersey canceled 92 %, and Philadelphia International Airport cut 80 %. The region is expected to continue suffering cancellations on Tuesday, with over 2,000 flights into or out of the U.S. projected to be canceled, particularly at Boston and New York airports.

    [Original] French minister moves to block US envoy Kushner from government access
    ShareSaveIan AikmanShareSaveEPACharles Kusher was appointed US Ambassador to France last yearThe French foreign ministry says US envoy Charles Kushner should be blocked from access to the government, after he failed to explain comments about an alleged "rise" of violence in France. Kushner, father of Donald Trump's son-in-law Jared Kushner, had been summoned to meet French Foreign Minister Jean-Noel Barrot on Monday evening, but he did not attend. Instead, he said he had a prior engagement and sent a deputy from the US embassy. This prompted an angry response from the ministry, which accused Kushner of an "apparent failure to grasp the basic requirements of the ambassadorial mission" and said Barrot had requested Kushner "no longer be allowed direct access" to government ministers. The diplomatic row began when the US embassy to France posted comments on social media about the murder of far-right nationalist Quentin Deranque. The post warned that "violent left-wing extremism is on the rise" in France. Deranque, a 23-year-old maths student, died in hospital on 14 February - two days after being beaten by a gang of masked young men in Lyon, in an attack which ministers from France's centre-right government have blamed on "far-left" militants. But the US embassy's comments appear to have angered the French government. "We reject any use of this tragedy, which has plunged a French family into mourning, for political ends," Barrot said over the weekend. "We have no lessons to learn, particularly on the issue of violence, from the international reactionary movement," he added. BBC News has approached the US embassy to France for comment. Six men suspected of being involved in the fatal assault have been charged over Deranque's death, while a parliamentary assistant for a deputy from the radical left France Unbowed (LFI) party has also been charged with complicity. LFI leader Jean-Luc MÃ©lenchon, who is a likely contender in next year's presidential election, has said his party had "nothing to do with this story" and condemned "all forms of violence". "We express our consternation, but also our empathy and compassion for [Deranque's] family and friends," he said.

    [Original] Briton among 19 killed in Nepal bus crash
    ShareSavePhanindra Dahal,BBC Nepali, in Kathmandu,andHafsa KhalilShareSaveEPAA 24-year-old British man is among 19 people who were killed in a bus crash in Nepal, police say. The bus - which had been carrying tourists - had been travelling to the capital, Kathmandu, when it lost control and fell 200m on to the bank of the Trishuli river, in the country's central Dhading district, in the early hours of Monday morning. There were 44 people onboard including the driver, 25 of whom suffered injuries. The bus had been travelling from Pokhara, a popular tourist spot. Nepal's Home Ministry has created a five-member taskforce to investigate the cause of the incident. The UK Foreign Office said it was assisting the family of the Briton who was killed. Nepalese authorities identified him as Stewart Dominic Ethan. His name has not been confirmed by the Foreign Office. Nepalese police say they have identified all 19 bodies, including a 40-year-old Chinese woman and a 32-year-old man from India. Among the injured is a Chinese national and a New Zealander. All the injured had been taken to hospitals in the capital, they added. Children were among those onboard. Multiple teams were sent to the site, including police units, the army and a rescue team of divers, authorities said. Police spokesman Abinarayan Kafle said 17 people died at the scene, with two more dying while receiving treatment, BBC Nepali reported. Road accidents are relatively commonplace in Nepal, due to a range of factors including poor road maintenance and narrow paths in mountainous areas. In 2024, at least 14 people died after a bus travelling from Pokhara to Kathmandu fell into the Marsyangdi river in the Tanahun district. "We are supporting the family of a British man who has died in Nepal and are in contact with the local authorities," a Foreign Office spokesman told the BBC. Nepal is a popular destination for many international visitors, especially climbers, who travel there to access a key section of the Himalaya mountain range that includes Mount Everest. Home to eight of the world's tallest peaks, mountaineering is a significant source of revenue for the country - in 2024, climbing fees brought in $5.9m (Â£4.4m).
